      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       This article is about the State of Delaware. For the indigenous people, see <a href="/wiki/Lenape" title="Lenape">Lenape</a>. For the bay, see <a href="/wiki/Delaware_Bay" title="Delaware Bay">Delaware Bay</a>. For the river, see <a href="/wiki/Delaware_River" title="Delaware River">Delaware River</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Delaware_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Delaware (disambiguation)">Delaware (disambiguation)</a>.
      </div>

        <a class="mw-kartographer-map" style="width: 265px; height: 225px;" data-mw="interface" data-style="osm-intl" data-width="265" data-height="225" data-zoom="6" data-overlays="[&quot;_af40e7221da4f84fbfb6d980a16a1a2e587725d6&quot;]" href="/wiki/Special:Map/6/a/a/en"><img src="https://maps.wikimedia.org/img/osm-intl,6,a,a,265x225.png?lang=en&amp;domain=en.wikipedia.org&amp;title=Delaware&amp;groups=_af40e7221da4f84fbfb6d980a16a1a2e587725d6" alt="" width="265" height="225" decoding="async" srcset="https://maps.wikimedia.org/img/osm-intl,6,a,a,265x225@2x.png?lang=en&amp;domain=en.wikipedia.org&amp;title=Delaware&amp;groups=_af40e7221da4f84fbfb6d980a16a1a2e587725d6 2x"></a>

        </div>

      <p><b>Delaware</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">ɛ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'w' in 'wind'">w</span><span title="/ɛər/: 'are' in 'bare'">ɛər</span></span>/</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Delaware.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e6/En-us-Delaware.ogg" class="internal" title="En-us-Delaware.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">DEL</span>-ə-wair</i></a>)<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> is a state in the <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_(United_States)" title="Mid-Atlantic (United States)">Mid-Atlantic</a><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> region of the United States, bordering <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a> to its south and west; <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a> to its north; and <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a> and the Atlantic Ocean to its east. The state takes its name from the nearby <a href="/wiki/Delaware_River" title="Delaware River">Delaware River</a> named after <a href="/wiki/Thomas_West,_3rd_Baron_De_La_Warr" title="Thomas West, 3rd Baron De La Warr">Thomas West, 3rd Baron De La Warr</a>, an English nobleman and <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>'s first colonial governor.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
      <p>Delaware occupies the northeastern portion of the <a href="/wiki/Delmarva_Peninsula" title="Delmarva Peninsula">Delmarva Peninsula</a> and some islands and territory within the Delaware River. It is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_and_territories_by_area" title="List of U.S. states and territories by area">second-smallest</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_and_territories_by_population" title="List of U.S. states and territories by population">sixth-least populous</a> state, but also the <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_and_territories_by_population_density" class="mw-redirect" title="List of U.S. states and territories by population density">sixth-most densely populated</a>. Delaware's largest city is <a href="/wiki/Wilmington,_Delaware" title="Wilmington, Delaware">Wilmington</a>, while the state capital is <a href="/wiki/Dover,_Delaware" title="Dover, Delaware">Dover</a>, the second-largest city in the state. The state is divided into <a href="/wiki/List_of_counties_in_Delaware" title="List of counties in Delaware">three counties</a>, having the lowest number of any state; from north to south, they are <a href="/wiki/New_Castle_County,_Delaware" title="New Castle County, Delaware">New Castle County</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kent_County,_Delaware" title="Kent County, Delaware">Kent County</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sussex_County,_Delaware" title="Sussex County, Delaware">Sussex County</a>. While the southern two counties have historically been predominantly agricultural, New Castle is more <a href="/wiki/Urbanized_area" class="mw-redirect" title="Urbanized area">urbanized</a>. Delaware's geography, culture, and history combine elements of the <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_(United_States)" title="Mid-Atlantic (United States)">Mid-Atlantic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States" title="Northeastern United States">Northeastern</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">Southern</a> regions of the country.</p>
      <p>Before its coastline was explored by <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Europe" title="Ethnic groups in Europe">Europeans</a> in the 16th century, Delaware was inhabited by several groups of Native Americans, including the <a href="/wiki/Lenape" title="Lenape">Lenape</a> in the north and <a href="/wiki/Nanticoke_Indian_Tribe" class="mw-redirect" title="Nanticoke Indian Tribe">Nanticoke</a> in the south. It was initially colonized by <a href="/wiki/Dutch_people" title="Dutch people">Dutch</a> traders at <a href="/wiki/Zwaanendael_Colony" title="Zwaanendael Colony">Zwaanendael</a>, near the present town of <a href="/wiki/Lewes,_Delaware" title="Lewes, Delaware">Lewes</a>, in 1631. Delaware was one of the <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">Thirteen Colonies</a> that took part in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>. On December 7, 1787, Delaware became the first state to ratify the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Constitution">Constitution of the United States</a>, and has since been known as <i>The First State</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Since the turn of the 20th century, Delaware is also a <i>de facto</i> onshore <a href="/wiki/Corporate_haven" title="Corporate haven">corporate haven</a>, in which by virtue of <a href="/wiki/Delaware_General_Corporation_Law" title="Delaware General Corporation Law">its corporate laws</a>, the state is the domicile of over 50% of all <a href="/wiki/New_York_Stock_Exchange" title="New York Stock Exchange">NYSE</a>-listed business and 60% of the <a href="/wiki/Fortune_500" title="Fortune 500"><i>Fortune</i> 500</a>.</p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Toponymy">Toponymy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Delaware&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Toponymy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
      <p>The state was named after the <a href="/wiki/Delaware_River#Origin_of_the_name" title="Delaware River">Delaware River</a>, which in turn derived its name from <a href="/wiki/Thomas_West,_3rd_Baron_De_La_Warr" title="Thomas West, 3rd Baron De La Warr">Thomas West, 3rd Baron De La Warr</a> (1577–1618) who was the ruling governor of the <a href="/wiki/Colony_of_Virginia" title="Colony of Virginia">Colony of Virginia</a> at the time Europeans first explored the river. The Delaware people, a name used by <a href="/wiki/Europeans" class="mw-redirect" title="Europeans">Europeans</a> for <a href="/wiki/Lenape" title="Lenape">Lenape</a> people indigenous to the Delaware Valley, also derive their name from the same source.</p>
      <p>The name <i>de La Warr</i> is from <a href="/wiki/Sussex" title="Sussex">Sussex</a> and of <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">Anglo</a>-French origin.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> It came probably from a <a href="/wiki/Norman_language" title="Norman language">Norman</a> <a href="/wiki/Lieu-dit" title="Lieu-dit">lieu-dit</a> <i>La Guerre</i>. This <a href="/wiki/Toponymic" class="mw-redirect" title="Toponymic">toponymic</a> could derive from <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a> <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ager#Latin" class="extiw" title="wikt:ager">ager</a></i>, from the <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">Breton</a> <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/gwern#Breton" class="extiw" title="wikt:gwern">gwern</a></i> or from the <a href="/wiki/Late_Latin" title="Late Latin">Late Latin</a> <i>varectum</i> (<a href="/wiki/Fallow" title="Fallow">fallow</a>). The toponyms Gara, Gare, Gaire (the sound <a href="/wiki/Open_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Open central unrounded vowel">[ä]</a> often mutated in <a href="/wiki/Near-open_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Near-open front unrounded vowel">[æ]</a>) also appear in old texts cited by <a href="/wiki/Lucien_Musset" title="Lucien Musset">Lucien Musset</a>, where the word <i>ga(i)ra</i> means <a href="/wiki/Gore_(road)" title="Gore (road)">gore</a>. It could also be linked with a <a href="/wiki/Patronymic" title="Patronymic">patronymic</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/verr#Old_Norse" class="extiw" title="wikt:verr">verr</a></i>.</p>
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Delaware&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
      <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096">
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Delaware" title="History of Delaware">History of Delaware</a>
      </div>

      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Native_Americans">Native Americans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Delaware&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Native Americans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
      <p>Before Delaware was settled by European colonists, the area was home to the Eastern <a href="/wiki/Algonquian_peoples" title="Algonquian peoples">Algonquian</a> tribes known as the Unami <a href="/wiki/Lenape" title="Lenape">Lenape</a>, or Delaware, who lived mostly along the coast, and the <a href="/wiki/Nanticoke_people" title="Nanticoke people">Nanticoke</a> who occupied much of the southern Delmarva Peninsula. John Smith also shows two Iroquoian tribes, the Kuskarawock and <a href="/wiki/Tockwogh" title="Tockwogh">Tockwogh</a>, living north of the Nanticoke—they may have held small portions of land in the western part of the state before migrating across the Chesapeake Bay.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The Kuskarawocks were most likely the <a href="/wiki/Tuscarora_people" title="Tuscarora people">Tuscarora</a>.</p>
      <p>The Unami Lenape in the Delaware Valley were closely related to <a href="/wiki/Munsee" title="Munsee">Munsee</a> Lenape tribes along the <a href="/wiki/Hudson_River" title="Hudson River">Hudson River</a>. They had a settled hunting and agricultural society, and they rapidly became middlemen in an increasingly frantic fur trade with their ancient enemy, the Minqua or <a href="/wiki/Susquehannock" title="Susquehannock">Susquehannock</a>. With the loss of their lands on the Delaware River and the destruction of the Minqua by the <a href="/wiki/Iroquois" title="Iroquois">Iroquois</a> of the Five Nations in the 1670s, the remnants of the Lenape who wished to remain identified as such left the region and moved over the <a href="/wiki/Alleghany_Mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Alleghany Mountains">Alleghany Mountains</a> by the mid-18th century. Generally, those who did not relocate out of the state of Delaware were baptized, became Christian and were grouped together with other persons of color in official records and in the minds of their non-Native American neighbors.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_Delaware">Colonial Delaware</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Delaware&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Colonial Delaware">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
      <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096">
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/New_Netherland" title="New Netherland">New Netherland</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Sweden" title="New Sweden">New Sweden</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Delaware_Colony" title="Delaware Colony">Delaware Colony</a>
      </div>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Dutch_people" title="Dutch people">Dutch</a> were the first Europeans to settle in present-day Delaware in the middle region by establishing a trading post at <a href="/wiki/Zwaanendael,_Delaware" class="mw-redirect" title="Zwaanendael, Delaware">Zwaanendael</a>, near the site of <a href="/wiki/Lewes,_Delaware" title="Lewes, Delaware">Lewes</a> in 1631.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Within a year all the settlers were killed in a dispute with <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">area Native American tribes</a>. In 1638 <a href="/wiki/New_Sweden" title="New Sweden">New Sweden</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Swedish_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Swedish people">Swedish</a> trading post and colony, was established at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Christina" title="Fort Christina">Fort Christina</a> (now in <a href="/wiki/Wilmington,_Delaware" title="Wilmington, Delaware">Wilmington</a>) by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Minuit" title="Peter Minuit">Peter Minuit</a> at the head of a group of Swedes, <a href="/wiki/Finnish_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Finnish people">Finns</a> and Dutch. The colony of New Sweden lasted 17 years. In 1651 the Dutch, reinvigorated by the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Peter_Stuyvesant" title="Peter Stuyvesant">Peter Stuyvesant</a>, established a fort at present-day <a href="/wiki/New_Castle,_Delaware" title="New Castle, Delaware">New Castle</a>, and in 1655 they conquered the New Sweden colony, annexing it into the Dutch <a href="/wiki/New_Netherland" title="New Netherland">New Netherland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-hod_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hod-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Only nine years later, in 1664, the Dutch were conquered by a fleet of English ships by Sir Robert Carr under the direction of <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_England" title="James II of England">James, the Duke of York</a>. Fighting off a prior claim by <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Calvert,_2nd_Baron_Baltimore" title="Cecil Calvert, 2nd Baron Baltimore">Cecil Calvert, 2nd Baron Baltimore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Maryland" title="Province of Maryland">Proprietor of Maryland</a>, the Duke passed his somewhat dubious ownership on to <a href="/wiki/William_Penn" title="William Penn">William Penn</a> in 1682. Penn strongly desired access to the sea for his <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Pennsylvania" title="Province of Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania province</a> and leased what then came to be known as the "Lower Counties on the Delaware"<sup id="cite_ref-hod_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hod-17">[17]</a></sup> from the Duke.</p>
      <p>Penn established representative government and briefly combined his two possessions under one General Assembly in 1682. However, by 1704 the Province of Pennsylvania had grown so large their representatives wanted to make decisions without the assent of the Lower Counties, and the two groups of representatives began meeting on their own, one at <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>, and the other at New Castle. Penn and his heirs remained proprietors of both and always appointed the same person Governor for their Province of Pennsylvania and their territory of the Lower Counties. The fact that Delaware and Pennsylvania shared the same governor was not unique. From 1703 to 1738 New York and New Jersey shared a governor.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Massachusetts and New Hampshire also shared a governor for some time.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
      <p>Dependent in early years on indentured labor, Delaware imported more slaves as the number of English immigrants decreased with better economic conditions in England. The colony became a slave society and cultivated tobacco as a cash crop, although English immigrants continued to arrive.</p>
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="American_Revolution">American Revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Delaware&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: American Revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
      <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096">
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lee_Resolution" title="Lee Resolution">Lee Resolution</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">United States Declaration of Independence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_campaign" title="Philadelphia campaign">Philadelphia campaign</a>, <a href="/wiki/Articles_of_Confederation#Ratification" title="Articles of Confederation">Articles of Confederation §&nbsp;Ratification</a>, <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783)" title="Treaty of Paris (1783)">Treaty of Paris (1783)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Convention_(United_States)" title="Constitutional Convention (United States)">Constitutional Convention (United States)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Admission_to_the_Union" title="Admission to the Union">Admission to the Union</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_by_date_of_admission_to_the_Union" title="List of U.S. states by date of admission to the Union">List of U.S. states by date of admission to the Union</a>
      </div>
      <p>Like the other middle colonies, the Lower Counties on the Delaware initially showed little enthusiasm for a break with <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Britain</a>. The citizenry had a good relationship with the Proprietary government, and generally were allowed more independence of action in their Colonial Assembly than in other colonies. Merchants at the port of Wilmington had trading ties with the British.</p>
      <p>So it was that New Castle lawyer <a href="/wiki/Thomas_McKean" title="Thomas McKean">Thomas McKean</a> denounced the <a href="/wiki/Stamp_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Stamp Act">Stamp Act</a> in the strongest terms, and Kent County native <a href="/wiki/John_Dickinson_(delegate)" class="mw-redirect" title="John Dickinson (delegate)">John Dickinson</a> became the "Penman of the Revolution." Anticipating the Declaration of Independence, <a href="/wiki/Patriot_(American_Revolution)" title="Patriot (American Revolution)">Patriot</a> leaders Thomas McKean and <a href="/wiki/Caesar_Rodney" title="Caesar Rodney">Caesar Rodney</a> convinced the Colonial Assembly to declare itself separated from British and Pennsylvania rule on June 15, 1776. The person best representing Delaware's majority, <a href="/wiki/George_Read_(signer)" class="mw-redirect" title="George Read (signer)">George Read</a>, could not bring himself to vote for a Declaration of Independence. Only the dramatic overnight ride of Caesar Rodney gave the delegation the votes needed to cast Delaware's vote for independence.</p>
      <p>Initially led by <a href="/wiki/John_Haslet" title="John Haslet">John Haslet</a>, Delaware provided one of the premier regiments in the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Army" title="Continental Army">Continental Army</a>, known as the "Delaware Blues" and nicknamed the "<a href="/wiki/Blue_Hen_Chicken" class="mw-redirect" title="Blue Hen Chicken">Blue Hen's Chicks</a>". In August 1777 <a href="/wiki/William_Howe,_5th_Viscount_Howe" title="William Howe, 5th Viscount Howe">General Sir William Howe</a> led a British army through Delaware on his way to a victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Brandywine" title="Battle of Brandywine">Battle of Brandywine</a> and capture of the city of Philadelphia. The only real engagement on Delaware soil was the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cooch%27s_Bridge" title="Battle of Cooch's Bridge">Battle of Cooch's Bridge</a>, fought on September 3, 1777, at <a href="/wiki/Cooch%27s_Bridge" title="Cooch's Bridge">Cooch's Bridge</a> in New Castle County, although there was a <a href="/wiki/Clow_Rebellion" title="Clow Rebellion">minor Loyalist rebellion</a> in 1778.</p>
      <p>Following the Battle of Brandywine, Wilmington was occupied by the British, and <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Delaware" class="mw-redirect" title="Governor of Delaware">State President</a> <a href="/wiki/John_McKinly" title="John McKinly">John McKinly</a> was taken prisoner. The British remained in control of the Delaware River for much of the rest of the war, disrupting commerce and providing encouragement to an active <a href="/wiki/Loyalist_(American_Revolution)" title="Loyalist (American Revolution)">Loyalist</a> portion of the population, particularly in Sussex County. Because the British promised slaves of rebels freedom for fighting with them, escaped slaves flocked north to join their lines.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
      <p>Following the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>, statesmen from Delaware were among the leading proponents of a strong central United States with equal representation for each state.</p>
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Slavery_and_race">Slavery and race</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Delaware&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Slavery and race">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
      <p>Many colonial settlers came to Delaware from Maryland and Virginia, where the population had been increasing rapidly. The economies of these colonies were chiefly based on labor-intensive tobacco and increasingly dependent on African <a href="/wiki/Slaves" class="mw-redirect" title="Slaves">slaves</a> because of a decline in working class immigrants from England. Most of the English colonists had arrived as <a href="/wiki/Indentured_servant" class="mw-redirect" title="Indentured servant">indentured servants</a> (contracted for a fixed period to pay for their passage), and in the early years the line between servant and slave was fluid.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
      <p>Most of the free African-American families in Delaware before the Revolution had migrated from Maryland to find more affordable land. They were descendants chiefly of relationships or marriages between white servant women and enslaved, servant or free African or African-American men.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Under slavery law, children took the social status of their mothers, so children born to white women were free, regardless of their paternity, just as children born to enslaved women were born into slavery. As the flow of indentured laborers to the colony decreased with improving economic conditions in England, more slaves were imported for labor and the caste lines hardened.</p>
      <p>By the end of the colonial period, the number of enslaved people in Delaware began to decline. Shifts in the agriculture economy from tobacco to mixed farming resulted in less need for slaves' labor. In addition local <a href="/wiki/Methodist" class="mw-redirect" title="Methodist">Methodists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quaker" class="mw-redirect" title="Quaker">Quakers</a> encouraged slaveholders to free their slaves following the American Revolution, and many did so in a surge of individual manumissions for idealistic reasons. By 1810 three-quarters of all blacks in Delaware were free. When John Dickinson freed his slaves in 1777, he was Delaware's largest slave owner with 37 slaves. By 1860, the largest slaveholder owned 16 slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKolchin199478,_81–82_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKolchin199478,_81–82-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
      <p>Although attempts to abolish slavery failed by narrow margins in the legislature, in practical terms the state had mostly ended the practice. By the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census,_1860" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Census, 1860">1860 census</a> on the verge of the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a>, 91.7% of the black population were free;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKolchin199481–82_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKolchin199481–82-24">[24]</a></sup> 1,798 were slaves, as compared to 19,829 "free colored persons".<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
      <p>An independent black denomination was chartered in 1813 by freed slave <a href="/wiki/Peter_Spencer_(religious_leader)" title="Peter Spencer (religious leader)">Peter Spencer</a> as the "<a href="/wiki/Spencer_Churches" title="Spencer Churches">Union Church of Africans</a>". This followed the 1793 establishment in Philadelphia of the <a href="/wiki/African_Methodist_Episcopal_Church" title="African Methodist Episcopal Church">African Methodist Episcopal Church</a> by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Allen_(bishop)" title="Richard Allen (bishop)">Richard Allen</a>, which had ties to the Methodist Episcopal Church until 1816. Spencer built a church in Wilmington for the new denomination.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> This was renamed as the <a href="/wiki/African_Union_First_Colored_Methodist_Protestant_Church_and_Connection" class="mw-redirect" title="African Union First Colored Methodist Protestant Church and Connection">African Union First Colored Methodist Protestant Church and Connection</a>, more commonly known as the <a href="/wiki/A.U.M.P._Church" title="A.U.M.P. Church">A.U.M.P. Church</a>. In 1814, Spencer called for the first annual gathering, known as the <a href="/wiki/Big_August_Quarterly" title="Big August Quarterly">Big August Quarterly</a>, which continues to draw members of this denomination and their descendants together in a religious and cultural festival.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
      <p>Delaware voted against <a href="/wiki/Secession" title="Secession">secession</a> on January 3, 1861, and so remained in the Union. While most Delaware citizens who fought in the war served in the regiments of the state, some served in companies on the Confederate side in <a href="/wiki/Maryland_Civil_War_Confederate_units" class="mw-redirect" title="Maryland Civil War Confederate units">Maryland</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Virginia_Civil_War_units" title="List of Virginia Civil War units">Virginia</a> Regiments. Delaware is notable for being the only slave state from which no Confederate regiments or militia groups were assembled.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Delaware essentially freed the few slaves who were still in bondage shortly after the Civil War<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag needs further explanation. (June 2020)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> but rejected the <a href="/wiki/Thirteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Thirteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">13th</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fourteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">14th</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fifteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fifteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">15th</a> Amendments to the Constitution; the 13th Amendment was rejected on February 8, 1865, the 14th Amendment was rejected on February 8, 1867, and the 15th Amendment was rejected on March 18, 1869. Delaware officially ratified the 13th, 14th, and 15th amendments on February 12, 1901.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reconstruction_and_industrialization">Reconstruction and industrialization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Delaware&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Reconstruction and industrialization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
      <p>After the Civil War, Democratic governments led by the state's Bourbon aristocracy continued to dominate the state and imposed an explicitly white supremacist regime in the state. The Democratic legislatures declared blacks second-class citizens in 1866 and restricted their voting rights despite the Fifteenth Amendment, ensuring continued Democratic success throughout most of the nineteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
      <p>Beginning in the late nineteenth century, the Wilmington area grew into a manufacturing center. Investment in manufacturing in the city grew from $5.5&nbsp;million in 1860 to $44&nbsp;million in 1900.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The most notable manufacturer in the state was the <a href="/wiki/DuPont_(1802%E2%80%932017)" title="DuPont (1802–2017)">Du Pont Company</a>. Because of Wilmington's growth, local politicians from the city and New Castle County pressured the state government to adopt a new constitution providing the north with more representation. However, the subsequent 1897 constitution did not proportionally represent the north and continued to give the southern counties disproportionate influence.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
      <p>As manufacturing expanded, businesses became major players in state affairs and funders of politicians through families such as the Du Ponts. Republican <a href="/wiki/J._Edward_Addicks" title="J. Edward Addicks">John Addicks</a> attempted to buy a US Senate seat multiple times in a rivalry with the Du Ponts until the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Seventeenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Seventeenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Seventeenth Amendment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The allegiance of industries with the Republican party allowed them to gain control of the state's governorship throughout most of the twentieth century. The GOP ensured blacks could vote because of their general support for Republicans and thus undid restrictions on black suffrage.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
      <p>Delaware benefited greatly from World War I because of the state's large gunpowder industry. The Du Pont Company, the most dominant business in the state by WWI, produced an estimated 40% of all gunpowder used by the Allies during the war. It produced nylon in the state after the war and began investments into <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Additionally, the company invested heavily in the expansion of public schools in the state and colleges such as the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Delaware" title="University of Delaware">University of Delaware</a> in the 1910s and 1920s. This included primary and secondary schools for blacks and women.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Delaware suffered less during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> than other states, but the depression spurred further migration from the rural south to urban areas.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II_to_present">World War II to present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Delaware&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: World War II to present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
      <p>Like in World War I, the state enjoyed a big stimulus to its gunpowder and shipyard industries in World War II. New job opportunities during and after the war in the Wilmington area coaxed African Americans from the southern counties to move to the city. The proportion of blacks constituting the city's population rose from 15% in 1950 to over 50% by 1980.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The surge of black migrants to the north sparked white flight in which middle class whites moved from the city to suburban areas, leading to general segregation of Delaware's society. In the 1940s and 1950s, the state attempted to integrate its schools. The University of Delaware admitted its first black student in 1948, and local courts ruled that primary schools had to be integrated. Delaware's integration efforts partially inspired the US Supreme Court's decision in <a href="/wiki/Brown_v._Board_of_Education" title="Brown v. Board of Education">Brown v. Board of Education</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
      <p>However, integration only encouraged more white flight, and poor economic conditions for the black population led to some violence during the 1960s. Riots broke out in Wilmington in 1967 and again in <a href="/wiki/Wilmington_riot_of_1968" title="Wilmington riot of 1968">1968 in response to the assassination of Martin Luther King Jr</a> after which the National Guard occupied the city for nine months to prevent further violence.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
      <p>Since WWII, the state has been generally economically prosperous and enjoyed relatively high per capita income because of its location between major cities like Philadelphia, New York, and Washington, DC.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Its population grew rapidly, particularly in the suburbs in the north where New Castle county became an extension of the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_metropolitan_area" class="mw-redirect" title="Philadelphia metropolitan area">Philadelphia metropolitan area</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Americans, including migrants from Puerto Rico, and immigrants from Latin America flocked to the state. By 1990, only 50% of Delaware's population consisted of natives to the state.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Delaware&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
      <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096">
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Twelve-Mile_Circle" title="Twelve-Mile Circle">Twelve-Mile Circle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wedge_(border)" title="Wedge (border)">Wedge (border)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mason%E2%80%93Dixon_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="Mason–Dixon Line">Mason–Dixon Line</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Transpeninsular_Line" title="Transpeninsular Line">Transpeninsular Line</a>
      </div>

      </div>
      <p>Delaware is 96 miles (154&nbsp;km) long and ranges from 9 miles (14&nbsp;km) to 35 miles (56&nbsp;km) across, totaling 1,982 square miles (5,130&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>),<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> making it the second-smallest state in the United States after <a href="/wiki/Rhode_Island" title="Rhode Island">Rhode Island</a>. Delaware is bounded to the north by <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>; to the east by the <a href="/wiki/Delaware_River" title="Delaware River">Delaware River</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delaware_Bay" title="Delaware Bay">Delaware Bay</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a> and the Atlantic Ocean; and to the west and south by <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>. Small portions of Delaware are also situated on the eastern side of the Delaware River sharing land boundaries with New Jersey. The state of Delaware, together with the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Shore_of_Maryland" title="Eastern Shore of Maryland">Eastern Shore counties of Maryland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Shore_of_Virginia" title="Eastern Shore of Virginia">two counties of Virginia</a>, form the <a href="/wiki/Delmarva_Peninsula" title="Delmarva Peninsula">Delmarva Peninsula</a>, which stretches down the Mid-Atlantic Coast.</p>
      <p>The definition of the northern boundary of the state is unusual. Most of the boundary between Delaware and Pennsylvania was originally defined by an arc extending 12 miles (19.3&nbsp;km) from the <a href="/wiki/Cupola" title="Cupola">cupola</a> of the courthouse in the city of <a href="/wiki/New_Castle,_Delaware" title="New Castle, Delaware">New Castle</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Need supporting reference for this claim (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This boundary is often referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Twelve-Mile_Circle" title="Twelve-Mile Circle">Twelve-Mile Circle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[a]</a></sup> Although the Twelve-Mile Circle is often claimed to be the only territorial boundary in the U.S. that is a true <a href="/wiki/Arc_(geometry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Arc (geometry)">arc</a>, the Mexican boundary with Texas includes several arcs,<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> and many cities in the South (such as <a href="/wiki/Plains,_Georgia" title="Plains, Georgia">Plains, Georgia</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup> also have circular boundaries.</p>
      <p>This border extends all the way east to the low-tide mark on the New Jersey shore, then continues south along the shoreline until it again reaches the 12-mile (19&nbsp;km) arc in the south; then the boundary continues in a more conventional way in the middle of the main channel (<a href="/wiki/Thalweg" title="Thalweg">thalweg</a>) of the Delaware River.</p>
      <p>To the west, a portion of the arc extends past the easternmost edge of Maryland. The remaining western border runs slightly east of due south from its intersection with the arc. <a href="/wiki/The_Wedge_(border)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Wedge (border)">The Wedge</a> of land between the northwest part of the arc and the Maryland border was claimed by both Delaware and Pennsylvania until 1921, when Delaware's claim was confirmed.</p>
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Topography">Topography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Delaware&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Topography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
      <p>Delaware is on a level plain, with the lowest mean elevation of any state in the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-census_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census-46">[45]</a></sup> Its highest elevation, located at <a href="/wiki/Ebright_Azimuth" title="Ebright Azimuth">Ebright Azimuth</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Concord_High_School_(Wilmington,_Delaware)" class="mw-redirect" title="Concord High School (Wilmington, Delaware)">Concord High School</a>, is less than 450 feet (140&nbsp;m) above sea level.<sup id="cite_ref-census_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census-46">[45]</a></sup> The northernmost part of the state is part of the <a href="/wiki/Piedmont_(United_States)" title="Piedmont (United States)">Piedmont Plateau</a> with hills and rolling surfaces.</p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Seaboard_fall_line" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlantic Seaboard fall line">Atlantic Seaboard fall line</a> approximately follows the <a href="/wiki/Delaware_Route_2" title="Delaware Route 2">Robert Kirkwood Highway</a> between <a href="/wiki/Newark,_Delaware" title="Newark, Delaware">Newark</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wilmington,_Delaware" title="Wilmington, Delaware">Wilmington</a>; south of this road is the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Coastal_Plain" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlantic Coastal Plain">Atlantic Coastal Plain</a> with flat, sandy, and, in some parts, swampy ground.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> A ridge about 75 to 80 feet (23 to 24&nbsp;m) high extends along the western boundary of the state and separates the <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">watersheds</a> that feed Delaware River and Bay to the east and the <a href="/wiki/Chesapeake_Bay" title="Chesapeake Bay">Chesapeake Bay</a> to the west.</p>
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Delaware&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
      <p>Since almost all of Delaware is a part of the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_coastal_plain" title="Atlantic coastal plain">Atlantic coastal plain</a>, the effects of the ocean moderate its climate. The state lies in the <a href="/wiki/Humid_subtropical_climate" title="Humid subtropical climate">humid subtropical climate</a> (<i>Cfa</i>) zone. Despite its small size (roughly 100 miles (160&nbsp;km) from its northernmost to southernmost points), there is significant variation in mean temperature and amount of snowfall between Sussex County and New Castle County. Moderated by the Atlantic Ocean and <a href="/wiki/Delaware_Bay" title="Delaware Bay">Delaware Bay</a>, the southern portion of the state has a milder climate and a longer growing season than the northern portion of the state. Delaware's all-time record high of 110&nbsp;°F (43&nbsp;°C) was recorded at <a href="/wiki/Millsboro,_Delaware" title="Millsboro, Delaware">Millsboro</a> on July 21, 1930. The all-time record low of −17&nbsp;°F (−27&nbsp;°C) was also recorded at Millsboro, on January 17, 1893. The <a href="/wiki/Hardiness_zone" title="Hardiness zone">hardiness zones</a> are 7a and 7b.</p>
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environment">Environment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Delaware&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Environment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
      <p>The transitional climate of Delaware supports a wide variety of vegetation. In the northern third of the state are found <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_coastal_forests" title="Northeastern coastal forests">Northeastern coastal forests</a> and mixed <a href="/wiki/Quercus" class="mw-redirect" title="Quercus">oak</a> forests typical of the northeastern United States.<sup id="cite_ref-ecoregions_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ecoregions-48">[47]</a></sup> In the southern two-thirds of the state are found <a href="/wiki/Middle_Atlantic_coastal_forests" title="Middle Atlantic coastal forests">Middle Atlantic coastal forests</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ecoregions_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ecoregions-48">[47]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Trap_Pond_State_Park" title="Trap Pond State Park">Trap Pond State Park</a>, along with areas in other parts of Sussex County, for example, support the northernmost stands of <a href="/wiki/Taxodium_distichum" title="Taxodium distichum">bald cypress</a> trees in North America.</p>
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_management">Environmental management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Delaware&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Environmental management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
      <p>Delaware provides <a href="/wiki/Government_subsidy" class="mw-redirect" title="Government subsidy">government subsidy support</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Environmental_remediation" title="Environmental remediation">clean-up</a> of property "lightly contaminated" by <a href="/wiki/Hazardous_waste" title="Hazardous waste">hazardous waste</a>, the proceeds for which come from a tax on wholesale petroleum sales.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup></p>
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Municipalities">Municipalities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Delaware&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Municipalities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
      <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096">
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Delaware_municipalities" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Delaware municipalities">List of Delaware municipalities</a>
      </div>
      <p>Wilmington is the state's most populous city (70,635) and its economic hub. It is located within commuting distance of both Philadelphia and Baltimore. Dover is the state capital and the second most populous city (38,079).</p>

      <p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census_Bureau" title="United States Census Bureau">United States Census Bureau</a> determined that the population of Delaware was 989,948 on April 1, 2020,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> an increase since the <a href="/wiki/2010_United_States_Census" class="mw-redirect" title="2010 United States Census">2010 United States Census</a> at 897,934.<sup id="cite_ref-PopEstUS_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PopEstUS-53">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup></p>
      <p>Delaware's history as a <a href="/wiki/Border_states_(Civil_War)" class="mw-redirect" title="Border states (Civil War)">border state</a> has led it to exhibit characteristics of both the <a href="/wiki/Northern_United_States" title="Northern United States">Northern</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">Southern</a> regions of the United States. Generally, the rural Southern (or "Slower Lower") regions of Delaware below the <a href="/wiki/Chesapeake_and_Delaware_Canal" class="mw-redirect" title="Chesapeake and Delaware Canal">Chesapeake and Delaware Canal</a> embody a <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_the_Southern_United_States" title="Culture of the Southern United States">Southern culture</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> while densely-populated Northern Delaware above the canal—particularly Wilmington, a part of the <a href="/wiki/Delaware_Valley" title="Delaware Valley">Philadelphia metropolitan area</a>—has more in common with that of the <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States" title="Northeastern United States">Northeast</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> The U.S. Census Bureau designates Delaware as one of the <a href="/wiki/South_Atlantic_States" class="mw-redirect" title="South Atlantic States">South Atlantic States</a>, but it is commonly associated with the <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Mid-Atlantic States">Mid-Atlantic States</a> and/or Northeastern United States by other federal agencies, the media, and some residents.<sup id="cite_ref-LOC_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LOC-58">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EPA_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPA-59">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FBI_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FBI-60">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-amtrak_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amtrak-61">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-princeton_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-princeton-62">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup></p>
      <p>Delaware is the sixth most densely populated state, with a population density of 442.6 people per square mile, 356.4 per square mile more than the national average, and ranking 45th in population. Delaware is one of five U.S. states (Maine, Vermont, West Virginia, Wyoming) that do not have a single city with a population over 100,000 as of the 2010 census.<sup id="cite_ref-Census_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Census-64">[63]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Center_of_population" title="Center of population">center of population</a> of Delaware is in New Castle County, in the town of <a href="/wiki/Townsend,_Delaware" title="Townsend, Delaware">Townsend</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cenpopcenter_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cenpopcenter-65">[64]</a></sup></p>
      <p>As of 2011<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Delaware&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, 49.7% of Delaware's population younger than one year of age belonged to minority groups (i.e., did not have two parents of non-Hispanic white ancestry).<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> In 2000 approximately 19% of the population were African-American and 5% of the population is Hispanic (mostly of Puerto Rican or Mexican ancestry).<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup></p>
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Race_and_ethnicity">Race and ethnicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Delaware&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Race and ethnicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
      <p>According to the 2010 United States census, the racial composition of the state was 68.9% <a href="/wiki/White_American" class="mw-redirect" title="White American">White American</a> (65.3% <a href="/wiki/Non-Hispanic_White" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-Hispanic White">Non-Hispanic White</a>, 3.6% <a href="/wiki/White_Hispanic" class="mw-redirect" title="White Hispanic">White Hispanic</a>), 21.4% <a href="/wiki/African_American" class="mw-redirect" title="African American">Black</a> or African American, 0.5% <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">American Indian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Native" class="mw-redirect" title="Alaska Native">Alaska Native</a>, 3.2% Asian American, 0.0% <a href="/wiki/Native_Hawaiian" class="mw-redirect" title="Native Hawaiian">Native Hawaiian</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Islander" title="Pacific Islander">Pacific Islander</a>, 3.4% some other race, and 2.7% <a href="/wiki/Multiracial_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Multiracial American">Multiracial American</a>. Ethnically, <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_and_Latino_Americans" title="Hispanic and Latino Americans">Hispanics</a> and Latin Americans of any race made up 8.2% of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> The 2019 <a href="/wiki/American_Community_Survey" title="American Community Survey">American Community Survey</a> estimated the state had a racial and ethnic makeup of 61.% non-Hispanic whites, 23.2% Black or African American, 0.7% American Indian or Alaska Native, 4.1% Asian, 0.1% Pacific Islander, 2.7% multiracial, and 9.6% Hispanic or Latin American of any race.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup></p>
      <p>In the Native American community, the state has a Native American group (called in their own language <a href="/wiki/Lenni_Lenape" class="mw-redirect" title="Lenni Lenape">Lenni Lenape</a>) which was influential in the colonial period of the United States and is today headquartered in Cheswold, Kent County, Delaware.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup> A band of the Nanticoke tribe of American Indians today resides in Sussex County and is headquartered in Millsboro, Sussex County, Delaware.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup></p>

      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Delaware&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
      <p>As of 2000, 91% of Delaware residents of age<span class="nowrap">&nbsp;</span>5 and older spoke only English at home; 5% spoke Spanish. French was the third-most spoken language at 0.7%, followed by Chinese at 0.5% and German at 0.5%.</p>
      <p>Legislation had been proposed in both the House and the Senate in Delaware to designate English as the <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_United_States" title="Languages of the United States">official language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup> Neither bill was passed in the legislature.</p>
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_orientation">Sexual orientation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Delaware&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Sexual orientation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
      <p>A 2012 Gallup poll found that Delaware's proportion of lesbian, gay, bisexual, and transgender adults stood at 3.4 percent of the population. This constitutes a total LGBT adult population estimate of 23,698 people. The number of same-sex couple households in 2010 stood at 2,646. This grew by 41.65% from a decade earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The citation is not specific enough to facilitate source verification (November 2013)">not specific enough to verify</span></a></i>]</sup> On July 1, 2013, same-sex marriage was legalized, and all civil unions would be converted into marriages.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup></p>
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Delaware&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

      </div>
      <p>As of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Delaware&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Delaware is mostly <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christian</a>. Although <a href="/wiki/Protestants" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestants">Protestants</a> account for almost half of the population,<sup id="cite_ref-pew2014_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pew2014-86">[85]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> is the largest single denomination in the state. The Association of Religion Data Archives<sup id="cite_ref-www.thearda.com_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-www.thearda.com-87">[86]</a></sup> reported in 2010 that the three largest denominational groups in Delaware by number of adherents are the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> at 182,532 adherents, the <a href="/wiki/United_Methodist_Church" title="United Methodist Church">United Methodist Church</a> with 53,656 members reported, and non-denominational <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Evangelical Protestant">Evangelical Protestant</a> with 22,973 adherents reported. The religious body with the largest number of congregations is the United Methodist Church (with 158 congregations) followed by non-denominational Evangelical Protestant (with 106 congregations), then the Catholic Church (with 45 congregations).</p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Diocese_of_Wilmington" title="Roman Catholic Diocese of Wilmington">Roman Catholic Diocese of Wilmington</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_Diocese_of_Delaware" title="Episcopal Diocese of Delaware">Episcopal Diocese of Delaware</a> oversee the parishes within their denominations. The <a href="/wiki/A.U.M.P._Church" title="A.U.M.P. Church">A.U.M.P. Church</a>, the oldest African-American denomination in the nation, was founded in Wilmington. It still has a substantial presence in the state. Reflecting new immigrant populations, an <a href="/wiki/Mosque" title="Mosque">Islamic mosque</a> has been built in the <a href="/wiki/Ogletown,_Delaware" title="Ogletown, Delaware">Ogletown</a> area, and a <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Temple_of_Delaware" title="Hindu Temple of Delaware">Hindu temple in Hockessin</a>.</p>
      <p>Delaware is home to an <a href="/wiki/Amish" title="Amish">Amish</a> community which resides west of <a href="/wiki/Dover,_Delaware" title="Dover, Delaware">Dover</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kent_County,_Delaware" title="Kent County, Delaware">Kent County</a>, consisting of nine church districts and about 1,650 people. The Amish first settled in Kent County in 1915. In recent years, increasing development has led to the decline in the number of Amish living in the community.<sup id="cite_ref-Elizabethtown_College_2018_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elizabethtown_College_2018-88">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-visitdoveramish_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-visitdoveramish-89">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-amishamerica_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amishamerica-90">[89]</a></sup></p>
      <p>A 2012 survey of religious attitudes in the United States found that 34% of Delaware residents considered themselves "moderately religious", 33% "very religious", and 33% as "non-religious".<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup> At the 2014 <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research</a> survey, 23% of the population were irreligious.</p>
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Delaware&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
      <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096">
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Delaware" title="Economy of Delaware">Economy of Delaware</a> and <a href="/wiki/Delaware_locations_by_per_capita_income" class="mw-redirect" title="Delaware locations by per capita income">Delaware locations by per capita income</a>
      </div>
      <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096">
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       For taxes, see <a href="#Taxation">§&nbsp;Taxation</a>.
      </div>
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Affluence">Affluence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Delaware&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Affluence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
      <table class="wikitable" style="float: right; margin: 10px">
       <caption>
        Average sale price for new &amp; existing homes (in U.S. dollars)<sup id="cite_ref-er2011_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-er2011-92">[91]</a></sup>

        <tr>
